2006 WL 3922159 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAYRA MORALES
FILE: AXX XX1 397 - SAN DIEGO
December 21, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Martin Zaehringer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kathryn E. Stuever
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler
  
APPLICATION: Remand
 
By an order dated August 23, 2006, an Immigration Judge found the respondent to have abandoned any and all claims for removal relief, and ordered her removal from the United States. The respondent filed a timely appeal of that decision, as well as a motion to remand. The respondent’s request for a waiver of the filing fee associated with the filing of an appeal is granted. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a)(1) and 1003.8(c). The motion will be granted and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
On appeal, the respondent asserts that her immigration proceedings should be reopened due to the ineffective assistance of her prior attorney, Mr. Harpreet S. Brar. More specifically, the respondent avers that but for the actions of Mr. Brar, she would not only have contested her single charge of removability, but she would also have submitted an alternative motion to withdraw her application for admission within the time limit set by the Immigration Judge. The respondent further argues that Mr. Brat failed to inform her of the Immigration Court’s decision, which hampered her ability to file the instant appeal.
 
Before making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the alien must ordinarily comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). Those requirements include: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved alien setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with her former counsel, (2) that the representative whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the respondent’s motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not. See id.; see also Siong v. INS, 376 F.3d 1030, 1036-37 (9th Cir. 2004). Moreover, the alien must also show prejudice from counsel’s alleged deficient performance. See Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785, 793 (9th Cir. 2005); Munoz v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 950, 955 (9th Cir.2003). Prejudice results when “the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Munoz v. Ashcroft, supra.
 
*2 Based upon the record before us, we find that the respondent has substantially complied with the Lozada requirements. See Respondent’s Brief, sub-exhs. 1-3. We further find that the record supports the respondent’s contention that she was prejudiced by the actions of her former counsel. For instance, Mr. Brar failed to meet any of the filing requirements set by the Immigration Judge, despite his written acknowledgment of those deadlines. See Exh. 5 (evidencing counsel’s awareness that he was to submit both the respondent’s motion to terminate proceedings and her withdrawal application for admission by July 20, 2006); see also Tr. at 10-17. See generally Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 582, 588 n.5 (9th Cir. 2006); Lin v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 1014, 1027 (9th Cir. 2004); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (finding ineffective assistance where counsel failed to file an application for relief for which the alien was prima facie eligible). The respondent’s prior attorney also failed to keep his client informed as to the status of her case. See generally Ray v. Gonzales, supra. Given the fact that she has been detained throughout these proceedings, we find that it was particularly incumbent upon Mr. Brar to keep in contact with the respondent. See generally Siong v. INS, supra. Thus, inasmuch as it appears that Mr. Brar’s failure to act denied the respondent an opportunity to establish her eligibility for any form of removal relief, we find that the respondent has suffered actual prejudice. Accordingly, we shall grant the respondent’s motion to reopen, and remand the record to the Immigration Court for additional proceedings.
 
In view of the foregoing, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.

2006 WL 3922153 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMED H.EL HEFNAWY
FILE: AXX XX3 762 - PHILADELPHIA
December 21, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David E. Piver, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated May 5, 2006. In our prior decision, we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen to apply for adjustment of status. The respondent now argues that the motion should have been granted due to the ineffectiveness of his former counsel and that the record should be remanded to permit him to apply for adjustment of status. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) does not oppose the motion, which will be denied.
 
We note that the respondent alleges that his appeal was untimely due to ineffective assistance of counsel. Specifically, he argues that his prior counsel failed to timely file a motion to reopen the Immigration Judge’s decision or to notify the respondent that he had failed to do so. As a result, the respondent’s voluntary departure period was not tolled and the respondent failed to leave at the end of that period, thereby rendering him ineligible for certain forms of relief for 10 years.
 
Even if we were to find that the respondent has met the requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel in this case, see Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556, 561-62 (BIA 2003), the respondent has not met the requirements for a motion to reopen for consideration of his adjustment of status application. In his motion, the respondent alleges that the visa petition filed on his behalf by his lawful permanent resident spouse is pending for adjudication with the DHS but has not yet been approved. Further, although the respondent’s spouse has applied for her United States citizenship, she is not yet a United States citizen and the respondent’s priority date is not yet current. Therefore, even upon approval of the respondent’s petition, there is not a visa immediately available to him. Accordingly, he is not prima facie eligible for adjustment of status. See, e.g., INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992) (an alien must make a prima facie showing both statutory eligibility for the relief sought and that relief is warranted in the exercise of discretion); Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413, 419 (BIA 1996) (an applicant demonstrates prima facie eligibility for relief where the evidence reveals a reasonable likelihood that the statutory requirements for relief have been satisfied).
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.

2006 WL 3922226 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GIUSEPPE AMOROSO A.K.A. JOE GUIDO
FILE: A18 850 801 - PHILADELPHIA
December 18, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Douglas A. Grannan, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of an aggravated felony
Sec. 241(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of a controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This matter has a long history before this Board, beginning with the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s February 2, 1998, decision in which he found the respondent, who on May 30, 1996, had been convicted of two counts of distribution of cocaine in violation of 21 U.S.C. § 841(a)(1), ineligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (repealed 1997); and ordered the respondent deported from the United States. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal from that decision by order dated December 6, 1999.
 
On September 11, 2006, when this matter was last before us, we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider our April 10, 2006, decision. In the April 10, 2006, decision, we denied his motion to reopen as time and number barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). In the instant motion, styled “Lozada Motion to Reopen,” the respondent argues that the proceedings should be reopened pursuant to INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001) to permit him to apply for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c).1 The respondent contends that he would have earlier applied for a waiver of inadmissibility under the St. Cyr decision had it not been for the ineffective assistance of his previous counsel. In support of his motion, the respondent provided a copy of the formal complaint that he has filed with the appropriate disciplinary authority against his former counsel, a copy of a letter notifying his former counsel of the complaint and giving him the opportunity to respond to the charges, and affidavits setting out the basis of his claim against his former counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Even if the respondent has satisfied the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, he does not benefit from the St. Cyr. decision and cannot therefore demonstrate prejudice. See Zheng v. Gonzales, 422 F.3d 98 (3rd Cir. 2005); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra. We find that the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim fails and the motion will be denied on that basis.
 
*2 In INS v. St. Cyr, supra, the Supreme Court held that discretionary relief under former § 212(c) “remains available for aliens … whose convictions were obtained through plea agreements and who … would have been eligible for § 212(c) relief at the time of their plea under the law then in effect.” See INS v. St. Cyr, supra, at 326. Here, the deportation proceedings were initiated against the respondent on March 27, 1997, with the filing of the Order to Show Cause (Form I-221) with the Immigration Court. The law in effect at the time of the respondent’s May 30, 1996, conviction was the Antiterrorism and Effective Death Penalty Act of 1996 (AEDPA), Pub. L. No. 104-132, 110 Stat. 1214), which was enacted on April 24, 1996. Thus, the respondent, who subsequent to April 24, 1996 was convicted of an aggravated felony, was not eligible for section 212(c) relief when his guilty plea was entered on May 30, 1996. See INS v. St. Cyr, supra; DiPeppe v. Quarantillo, 337 F.3d 326 (3rd Cir. 2003); Perez v. Elwood, 294 F.3d 552 (3d Cir. 2002); DeSousa v. Reno, 190 F.3d 175 (3d Cir. 1999). See also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44 (section 212(c) available where proceedings initiated before April 24, 1996).
 
In addition, the record shows that in late July 2001 or early August 2001, the respondent was deported from the United States. See Respondent’s Affidavit in support of the instant motion. He may not reopen the original immigration proceedings to apply for a waiver of deportation because he left the United States after those proceedings were initiated. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44(k)(2) (specifically providing that special motions to reopen are unavailable to aliens “issued a final order of deportation … who then illegally returned to the United States”). We note that the respondent did not state in his affidavit whether he has returned to the United States and if so, under what circumstances. In any event, the respondent’s departure deprived this Board of jurisdiction to reopen the proceedings.
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

1

Our records show that although the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) did not respond to the respondent’s motion to reconsider, the instant brief was fast filed herein as “Respondent’s Response to Government’s Opposition” on September 11, 2006, the same date that our previous decision was issued. Given the respondent’s expression of dissatisfaction with his former counsel’s performance in his brief and supporting affidavit, we noted the respondent’s failing to satisfy the procedural requirements of and demonstrate prejudice as required by Matter of Lozada.

2006 WL 3922166 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BORALDA ILIAS
FILE: AXX XX4 416 - DETROIT
December 18, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marshal E. Hyman, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on January 28, 2005, when we summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s denial of a motion to reopen an in absentia order of removal. On July 24, 2006, the respondent filed an untimely motion to reopen. The respondent contends that the time limitation on a motion to reopen, see 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), should be tolled and that her proceeding should be reopened due to ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent has complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Hamid v. Ashcroft, 336 F.3d 465, 469 (6th Cir. 2003). Further, the respondent has shown ineffective assistance by her former counsel. See Matter of Grijalva-Barrera, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996). The respondent avers that her former counsel specifically directed that she not appear at her initial master calendar hearing because he was seeking consolidation of her proceeding with that of her parents. The record contains a statement from the Department of Homeland Security anticipating that the respondent’s proceeding would be heard with her family (Exh. 3). However, the respondent’s former counsel never filed a motion to consolidate, and the record does not contain any other document indicating that a request to consolidate and/or transfer venue was filed or presented. In a motion to reopen/rescind filed with the Immigration Court, the respondent’s former counsel alleged that government counsel indicated that she would route the case so that it would be consolidated, but government counsel denied that she was the trial attorney at the respondent’s family’s master calendar hearings.
 
Given these facts and circumstances, we find that the actions and advice of the respondent’s former counsel constituted ineffective assistance and that the respondent has demonstrated due diligence in presenting her claim in this regard. Accordingly, the motion to reopen is granted. Further, our decision, as well as the Immigration Judge’s decisions, are vacated, and the record is remanded for further proceedings.

2006 WL 3712522 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KHALID UMER
FILE: AXX XX2 967 - SAN ANTONIO
December 8, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Omar Cura, Jr., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Anibal D. Martinez
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
  
APPLICATION: Section 212(c) waiver
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s July 22, 2005, order finding him removable, deeming his application for relief to be abandoned, and ordering him removed to Pakistan. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
At proceedings held on June 14, 2005, the respondent conceded removability as charged (Tr. at 15-16), and was directed by the Immigration Judge to file his application for relief in compliance with local procedures, noting that the court required strict adherence to those procedures. The Immigration Judge asked if there were any questions. Neither the respondent nor his counsel raised any questions concerning the court’s filing requirements. Nevertheless, the respondent’s application was not timely filed, and the Immigration Judge cancelled the merits hearing and entered an order of removal.
 
The respondent’s notice of appeal and brief list several explanations for the failure to timely file the application. One explanation was that there was bad advice from the clerk’s office as to when the application could be filed. This explanation was proffered in the form of a statement by counsel. Statements or assertions by counsel are not evidence. Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980). Nothing else has been submitted to support the respondent’s claim in this regard.
 
Counsel for the respondent further claims that the filing was too complex to be successfully accomplished in a timely fashion. The record does not support this claim. The transcript of record makes it clear that the Immigration Judge provided an opportunity for questions about filing and no objection was made to the scheduling of the merits hearing (Tr. at 17-18). It is clear from the record that the respondent did not object to the filing deadline.
 
The respondent also argues that the Immigration Judge caused the filing to be late by arbitrarily rejecting the filed application for minor technical reasons, such as counsel’s failure to mark the box indicating he was not subject to disciplinary procedures. The assertion that any action of the Immigration Judge caused the application to be late is not supported by the record. The respondent also argues that he should not suffer from his counsel’s mistakes. However, an ineffective assistance of counsel claim has not been made. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (setting forth the procedure for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim).
 
*2 In sum, we find no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the application was abandoned. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c) (providing that an Immigration Judge may set reasonable time limits for the filing of applications and the failure to file within the time set means the opportunity to file is deemed waived); Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547, 549-50 (BIA 1992); Matter of Jean, 17 I&N Dec. 100, 102 (BIA 1979).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

End of Document

2006 WL 3712528 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BEATRICE ATZEL
FILE: AXX XX3 990 - MIAMI
December 7, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Neil F. Lewis, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal; remand
 
The respondent has appealed from an Immigration Judge decision denying her request for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). She has also requested that we remand the record due to ineffective assistance of her previous counsel, for the presentation of new and material evidence, and based on her argument that the Notice to Appear was improper. The appeal will be sustained in part and the record remanded for further proceedings.
 
The respondent has for the most part compiled with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved applicant setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the applicant in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id at 640. While it is not entirely clear from the record whether the respondent’s previous attorney acted ineffectively to such an extent as to prejudice the respondent, the respondent has now presented significant additional evidence, some of which could have been produced at the previous hearing, perhaps indicating ineffective assistance, and some of which was unavailable, requiring that the record be remanded for consideration of the new evidence. In particular, we note the possible lapse of judgement on the part of previous counsel in not calling the respondent’s United States citizen husband to testify on the respondent’s behalf although he was present at the time. It appears he will suffer meaningful hardship were the respondent to be removed from the United States, a factor only briefly considered by the Immigration Judge in his decision. We also note that although the respondent, through previous counsel, admitted to the allegations against her, the charging document does, in fact, indicate an incorrect statute under which she was convicted.
 
*2 Given the problems outlined above, we find we should remand the record for further consideration of the respondent’s removability and her application for cancellation of removal.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2006 WL 3712528 (BIA)
End of Document

24 I. & N. Dec. 56 (BIA), Interim Decision 3548, 2006 WL 3540044
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE O-S-G-, RESPONDENT
Decided December 6, 2006
**1 *56 A motion to reconsider a decision of the Board of Immigration Appeals must include the following: (1) an allegation of material factual or legal errors in the prior decision that is supported by pertinent authority; (2) in the case of an affirmance without opinion (“AWO”), a showing that the alleged errors and legal arguments were previously raised on appeal and a statement explaining how the Board erred in affirming the Immigration Judge’s decision under the AWO regulations; and (3) if there has been a change in law, a reference to the relevant statute, regulation, or precedent and an explanation of how the outcome of the Board’s decision is materially affected by the change.
 
FOR RESPONDENT:
Brad Alexander, Esquire,
Miami, Florida
BEFORE: Board Panel: OSUNA, Acting Chairman; HOLMES and FILPPU, Board Members.
OSUNA, Acting Chairman:
Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b) (2006), the respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider our decision dated June 13, 2006, in which a Board Member summarily affirmed the decision of the Immigration Judge. In his motion to reconsider, the respondent restates in general terms his prior arguments and asserts for the first time that he will be persecuted on account of his membership in a particular social group. This latter claim was not raised in his Application for Asylum and for Withholding of Removal (Form I-589), at the hearing before the Immigration Judge, or in his brief on appeal. The respondent does not allege any factual or legal error in our prior order under the summary affirmance regulations. Nor does he refer to new legal authority that materially affects his case. The motion to reconsider will therefore be denied.
  
I. MOTIONS TO RECONSIDER
 
By statute and regulation, a motion to reconsider must state the reasons for the motion by specifying the errors of fact or law in a prior Board decision, and it must be supported by pertinent authority. Section 240(c)(6)(C) of the *57 Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C.A. § 1229a(c)(6)(C) (West 2005);1 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1).2 Only one motion to reconsider any given decision may be submitted, and it must be filed within 30 days after the mailing of the Board’s decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). In removal proceedings an alien may file only one motion to reconsider a decision that the alien is removable from the United States, and the motion must be filed within 30 days of the entry of a final administrative order of removal. Sections 240(c)(6)(A), (B) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2).3 A party may not file a motion to reconsider a previously denied motion to reconsider. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). A motion to reconsider is also barred if it is filed solely to challenge a decision to affirm without opinion by a single Board Member, or to adjudicate the case without a panel of three Board Members. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(3). However, we still retain authority at any time to reconsider on our own motion any case in which we have rendered a decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). In addition, we have authority to deny a motion to reconsider as a matter of discretion. Id.
 
**2 A motion to reconsider is a “‘request that the Board reexamine its decision in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or perhaps an argument or aspect of the case which was overlooked.”’ Matter of Ramos, 23 I&N Dec. 336, 338 (BIA 2002) (quoting Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 n.2 (BIA 1991)). A motion to reconsider challenges the Board’s original decision and alleges that it is defective in some regard. Matter of Cerna, supra, at 402. A motion to reconsider contests the correctness of the original decision based on the previous factual record, as opposed to a motion *58 to reopen, which seeks a new hearing based on new or previously unavailable evidence. Id. at 403; see also section 240(c)(7)(B) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
A motion to reconsider based on a legal argument that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied. Matter of Medrano, 20 I&N Dec. 216, 219 (BIA 1990, 1991) (“Arguments for consideration on appeal should all be submitted at one time, rather than in piecemeal fashion.”) A motion to reconsider is not a mechanism by which a party may file a new brief before the Board raising additional legal arguments that are unrelated to those issues raised before the Immigration Judge and on appeal. Rather, the “additional legal arguments” that may be raised in a motion to reconsider should flow from new law or a de novo legal determination reached by the Board in its decision that may not have been addressed by the parties. Similarly, a motion to reconsider is not a process by which a party may submit, in essence, the same brief presented on appeal and seek reconsideration by generally alleging error in the prior Board decision. The moving party must specify the factual and legal issues raised on appeal that were decided in error or overlooked in our initial decision or must show how a change in law materially affects our prior decision. For example, reconsideration may be granted if a movant informs us that a particular aspect of the case, already raised or addressed during the hearing, was not considered in adjudicating the appeal, see, e.g., Matter of Ramos, supra, or if an argument raised in the brief was overlooked at the time we entered our decision, see, e.g., Matter of Robles, 24 I&N Dec. 22 (BIA 2006).
  
II. STANDARDS FOR A MOTION TO RECONSIDER A DECISION TO AFFIRM WITHOUT OPINION
 
In 2002 the Attorney General enacted regulations to streamline case management at the Board. See Board of Immigration Appeals: Procedural Reforms To Improve Case Management, 67 Fed. Reg. 54,878 (Aug. 26, 2002); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (2006). These reform regulations included the expanded use of decisions by a single Board Member. The regulations permit a single Board Member to decide a case by use of a summary affirmance order, otherwise known as an affirmance without opinion or “AWO.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(4). By regulation, an AWO must state the following: “The Board affirms, without opinion, the result of the decision below. The decision below is, therefore, the final agency determination. See 8 CFR 3.1(e)(4).” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(4)(ii).4
 
**3 *59 The regulations regarding the use of an AWO state that a Board Member shall affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge without opinion if three conditions are met. First, the Board Member must determine that the result reached in the decision under review was correct. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(4)(i). Second, the Board Member must conclude that any errors in the decision under review were harmless or nonmaterial. Id. Third, the Board Member must find that
(A) The issues on appeal are squarely controlled by existing Board or federal court precedent and do not involve the application of precedent to a novel factual situation; or
 
(B) The factual and legal issues raised on appeal are not so substantial that the case warrants the issuance of a written opinion in the case.
 
Id. (emphasis added). While the regulations bar a motion to reconsider solely on the management decision to issue an AWO by a single Board Member, a party may still move to reconsider the underlying reasons for the affirmance of the Immigration Judge’s decision, if appropriate. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(3).
 
In order to properly seek reconsideration of a summary affirmance order, the moving party should set forth the reasons the Board erred in concluding that the Immigration Judge’s decision was correct. The motion should identify the material errors in the underlying Immigration Judge’s decision, if any, that were overlooked by the Board and that ultimately affected the disposition of the appeal. In the event there is a change in law, the movant should identify the precedent, statute, or regulation at issue and show how the change materially affects the outcome of the case. Similarly, if the Board overlooked controlling precedent, the motion should state succinctly which issues that were raised on appeal involved such precedent, and how the case law changes the outcome of the case. In addition, the movant must show that the factual or legal issues that were raised on appeal were substantial and should have been considered, and the movant must explain how the issues, if addressed, would have changed the disposition of the appeal. A motion to reconsider an AWO that fails to address these factors, as applicable, will be denied for failure to sufficiently articulate the reasons for reconsideration.
  
III. APPLICATION
 
In the instant case the respondent is seeking reconsideration of our summary affirmance order. The motion to reconsider essentially sets forth two bases for reconsideration. First, the motion reiterates the argument first raised on appeal that the respondent has a well-founded fear of persecution *60 based on political opinion. It does so, however, in very general terms, essentially by setting forth legal propositions without a detailed discussion of how those propositions apply to the respondent. Second, the respondent requests that we reconsider the summary affirmance in light of a completely new legal claim-that he was persecuted or has a well-founded fear of persecution on account of his alleged membership in a particular social group. He contends that this “additional legal argument” may properly be presented in a motion to reconsider. We disagree.
 
**4 The respondent failed to assert his claim regarding membership in a particular social group in either his Application for Asylum and Withholding of Removal or during the hearing before the Immigration Judge. Likewise, he did not raise this issue on appeal. Thus, the motion to reconsider fails to identify a material issue that was overlooked by the Board in affirming the decision of the Immigration Judge. Indeed, the motion to reconsider does not point to any material error in the decision of the Immigration Judge.
 
Furthermore, the motion to reconsider does not allege that the issues, as raised on appeal, involved the application of precedent to a novel situation, or that there is new precedent or a change in law that affects our prior decision. The respondent has also not asserted any new facts or provided new evidence for our consideration that would convert his motion to reconsider into a motion to reopen. Instead, the respondent generally reiterates prior arguments and raises completely new legal arguments, which were not previously raised either before the Immigration Judge or on appeal, and which are based on the same factual record. See Matter of Medrano, supra. As noted above, a motion to reconsider must include specific allegations as to how the Board erred as a matter of fact or law in its AWO decision, and it must be supported by pertinent legal authority. Because the respondent has failed to raise such allegations of error in his motion to reconsider, we will deny the motion.
  
IV. CONCLUSION
 
In conclusion, we find that a motion to reconsider should, consistent with the statute and regulations set forth at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b), include the following: (1) an allegation of material factual or legal errors in the Board’s decision that is supported by pertinent authority; (2) if the Board summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision, a showing that the alleged errors and legal arguments were previously raised on appeal and a statement explaining how the Board erred in affirming the Immigration Judge’s decision under the AWO regulations; and (3) if there has been a change in law, a reference to the relevant statute, regulation, or precedent and an explanation of how the outcome of the Board’s decision is materially affected by the change. We find that the respondent’s motion fails to assert any factual or *61 legal errors in our prior decision under the AWO regulations. Accordingly, the motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.

See sections 240(c)(6) and (7) of the Act; INS v. Jong Ha Wang, 450 U.S. 139, 140-41 (1981). In addition to the provisions of the statute and the regulations, a number of precedent decisions provide guidance on the filing of substantive motions. See, e.g., Matter of C-C-, 23 I&N Dec. 899 (BIA 2006) (reopening for asylum based on a change in circumstances and country conditions); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel); Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002) (reopening for adjustment of status based on a marriage entered into after the commencement of removal proceedings); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel).

Compare sections 240(c)(6)(A), (B) of the Act with 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2) and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (2006). See generally Matter of Goolcharan, 23 I&N Dec. 5 (BIA 2001); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).

2006 WL 3712433 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN RAMIREZ-VAZQUEZ
FILE: AXX XX0 272 - SAN DIEGO
November 28, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrea Guerrero, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Monica Mubaraki
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
This matter was last before the Board on May 24, 2006, when we granted the respondent’s motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In that order, we noted that the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) did not oppose the motion. The DHS has filed a timely motion to reconsider contending that it did indeed oppose the respondent’s motion, as reflected in its “Opposition to Respondent’s Motion to Reopen” (“Opposition”) dated April 24, 2006. The respondent opposes the motion to reconsider.
 
Notwithstanding the parties’ differing views on whether the DHS’ April 24, 2006, Opposition was properly filed and should now be considered, we have decided to grant the DHS’ request for reconsideration of our May 24, 2006, decision. Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is granted. Upon reconsideration, we once again grant the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
We have reviewed the material submitted in conjunction with the respondent’s motion to reopen and find that he has satisfied all of the requirements, as discussed in Matter of Lozada, supra, for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his former attorney. Further, we observe that at the time of his hearing before the Immigration Judge on February 7, 2005, (when he was represented by his former attorney), the respondent had already lived in the United States for over 15 1/2 years and had four qualifying relatives, including his lawful permanent resident mother and three United States citizen children. At a minimum, these facts warranted a discussion between the respondent and his attorney as to whether he could qualify for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. The respondent maintains that his former attorney never advised him of the availability of such relief in his case. As such the line of inquiry regarding the relevant hardship facts was cut off. The failure of the respondent’s former counsel to inform the respondent even of the possibility of section 240A(b) relief in his case amounts to ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Nothing in the DHS’ Opposition persuades us to deny the respondent’s Lozada motion. The DHS asserts that the respondent’s former attorney simply concluded that the respondent could not demonstrate the necessary degree of hardship for purposes of establishing eligibility for cancellation of removal, particularly given that many of the hardship factors set forth in the respondent’s motion did not exist at the time of his hearing before the Immigration Judge. This argument fails because it is purely conjectural in nature. There is no statement from the respondent’s former attorney in the record, and therefore there is no basis for determining what he concluded about the respondent’s eligibility for cancellation of removal. Further, there has been no showing that the respondent’s former attorney ever informed him of the option of applying for cancellation of removal, much less discussed with him the specific elements for demonstrating eligibility for such relief.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The DHS’ motion to reconsider is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen filed with this Board April 7, 2006, is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s request for a stay of his removal order is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and the entry of a new decision.

2006 WL 3712502 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARIE JARAN SEVILLA-PAZ
FILE: AXX XX8 992 - DALLAS
November 21, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joseph Lopez-Wilson, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 14, 2006, denying his motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for his failure to appear at the hearing on June 5, 2000. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that he did not receive notice of his hearing. He claims that the Notice to Appear (Form I-862) was mailed to an address where the respondent allegedly lived, but he never received any notice of the hearing. The respondent submitted an affidavit with his motion to reopen, in which he states that one day prior to his hearing he contacted his former attorney and he was informed of his scheduled hearing date. However, he indicates that he did not have the hearing notice with him, and therefore, he was unable to find the Immigration Court in Dallas, Texas. He claims that a guard at one of the downtown buildings in Dallas told him to wait for another appointment letter. The respondent requests that the proceedings be reopened based on lack of notice and ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent also claims that he is eligible for asylum and he has an approved visa petition that was filed on his behalf by his brother.
 
The record reflects that the Notice to Appear was served personally on the respondent on November 28, 1999, and he was informed in the Spanish language of the consequences of a failure to appear. The respondent also signed the Notice to Appear acknowledging his receipt of it. On April 6, 2000, the hearing notice was sent to the respondent’s former attorney of record, Thelma O. Garcia. The Immigration Judge noted that on the day of the respondent’s master calendar hearing, June 5, 2000, the Immigration Court received a motion to withdraw as attorney of record from Ms. Garcia. The Immigration Judge indicates that he denied Ms. Garcia’s request to withdraw as the attorney of record, and he formally singed the denial order on June 6, 2000. The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia for his failure to appear on June 5, 2000.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that proper notice was provided to the respondent. The record reflects that the notice of hearing was sent to the respondent’s former attorney of record on April 6, 2000. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). Moreover, the respondent indicates that he was aware of his scheduled heating on June 5, 2000, but he was unable to locate the Dallas Immigration Court on the day of the hearing. On appeal the respondent asserts a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. However, he has not complied with any of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). We find that based upon the evidence of record, the respondent received notice of the hearing in accordance with the Act, and he has not met the burden of demonstrating lack of notice under the statute. The respondent also has not established that he failed to appear due to exceptional circumstances, based on ineffective assistance of counsel. See also Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998) (claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception to the statutory requirement that motion to reopen be filed within 180 days of in absentia order). Nor has the respondent explained why he did not seek reopening until almost six years after being ordered removed in absentia. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2006 WL 3712506 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PETER KWAKA YEBOAH
FILE: AXX XX5 635 - DENVER
November 16, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Osei Prempeh, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Cathy Auble
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent, a Citizen of Ghana, was ordered removed from the United States on February 3, 2005, because he failed to appear for his hearing. He filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on August 2, 2005, but the Immigration Judge denied the request on August 22, 2005. That decision was not appealed to the Board. The respondent filed a second motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on March 28, 2006. The motion was denied on May 3, 2006, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent argued in his second motion to reopen and on appeal that these proceedings should be reopened because he is the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. According to the respondent, his prior attorney neglected to appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his first motion to reopen, and thereby denied him due process in these proceedings.
 
The regulation at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) provides that an alien may file only one motion to seek reopening of an order of removal entered in absentia. The respondent’s second motion to reopen is numerically-barred from consideration. In addition, we note that the Notice of Hearing was mailed to the respondent at 1885 S. Quebec Way, #1-25, Denver, Colorado 80231, but was returned to the Immigration Court because the respondent no longer resided at the address. We further note that the respondent received the Notice to Appear at that address on November 2, 2004, and apparently changed his address thereafter, but did not notify the Immigration Court of the change. We therefore find that the respondent was properly notified of the hearing. Having found that the respondent received proper notice of the hearing, we find that the respondent has not suffered prejudice on account of his first counsel’s alleged failure to appeal the denial of the respondent’s first motion to reopen. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
Additionally, the respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, as set forth in Matter of Assaad, supra, and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA1988); see also Galvez Pineda v. Gonzales, 127 F.3d 833 (10th Cir. 2005) (recognizing requirements for filing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim with Board). The respondent has provided no proof that his prior counsel was notified of the allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel, and provided an opportunity to respond to the respondent’s second motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge’s decision is also incorporated into this order, and is affirmed.
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2006 WL 3712705 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RITA NWAMAKA ISIMA
FILE: AXX XX2 913 - ATLANTA
November 15, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lina T. Hernandez,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 27, 2006. The respondent failed to appear for her hearing on March 10, 2004. She was ordered to be removed in proceedings conducted in absentia. Nearly 2 years later, on January 4, 2006, the respondent submitted a motion to reopen based on a lack of notice. She claimed that she informed her former attorney of her change of address, but the Notice to Appear was sent to her prior address and she did not receive it. She stated that the illness of one relative in 2001, and the death of another in 2002, caused her to lack involvement with her immigration matters, and are exceptional circumstances. The respondent said that she was not aware that her former counsel had withdrawn her application for adjustment of status. She claimed that she is eligible for cancellation of removal. The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent did not inform the Department of Homeland Security (formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) of her change of address, and found that the Notice to Appear and the hearing notice were properly sent to her last known address. The Immigration Judge found no connection between the respondent’s failure to appear in 2004, and the illness of her relative commencing in 2001, or the death of another relative in 2002. The Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent had not demonstrated that she was prevented from appearing due to exceptional circumstances to warrant reopening.
 
On appeal, the respondent claims ineffective assistance from both the attorney who represented her on her motion to reopen and from her former attorney. The respondent has not presented a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel that is compliant with our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). She has not shown that the attorneys whose integrity or competence is being impugned have been informed of the allegations leveled against them and given an opportunity to respond. Further, the respondent has not shown that a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of the counsels’ ethical or legal responsibilities, and why such a complaint or complaints have not been filed. The Immigration Judge appropriately denied reopening. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

2006 WL 3712710 (BIA)

IN RE: EMMANUEL ISIMA OKECHUKWU A.K.A. EMANUAL ISIMA OKECHUKWU

November 15, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lina T. Hernandez,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 27, 2006. The respondent failed to appear for his hearing on March 10, 2004. He was ordered to be removed in proceedings conducted in absentia. Nearly 2 years later, on January 4, 2006, the respondent submitted a motion to reopen based on a lack of notice. He claimed that he informed his former attorney of his change of address, but the Notice to Appear was sent to his prior address and he did not receive it. He stated that the illness of one relative in 2001, and the death of another in 2002, caused him to lack involvement with his immigration matters, and are exceptional circumstances. The respondent said that he was not aware that his former counsel had withdrawn his application for adjustment of status. He claimed that he is eligible for cancellation of removal. The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent did not inform the Department of Homeland Security (formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) of his change of address, and found that the Notice to Appear and the hearing notice were properly sent to his last known address. The Immigration Judge found no connection between the respondent’s failure to appear in 2004, and the illness of his relative commencing in 2001, or the death of another relative in 2002. The Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent had not demonstrated that he was prevented from appearing due to exceptional circumstances to warrant reopening.
 
On appeal, the respondent claims ineffective assistance from both the attorney who represented him on his motion to reopen and from his former attorney. The respondent has not presented a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel that is compliant with our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). He has not shown that the attorneys whose integrity or competence is being impugned have been informed of the allegations leveled against them and given an opportunity to respond. Further, the respondent has not shown that a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of the counsels’ ethical or legal responsibilities, and why such a complaint or complaints have not been filed. The Immigration Judge appropriately denied reopening. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2006 WL 3485802 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIETH VAN BREDA STRETTEN A.K.A. MARIETH VAN BREDA
FILE: AXX XX9 723 - DETROIT
November 7, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Bruce G. Davis, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kathleen L. Alcorn
Chief Counsel
*1 On May 17, 2006, an Immigration Judge granted the respondent voluntary departure in lieu of removal. The respondent has appealed. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
The respondent is married to a United States citizen. The respondent alleges that she and her husband contracted with her former attorney, Mr. Casterline, to file a petition for an immediate relative visa on her behalf. The respondent further alleges that although the immigration Judge granted her several continuances so that Mr. Casterline could file the petition, he failed to do so, and, as a consequence, she was told to accept voluntary departure in lieu of removal. Faced with an order of removal, the respondent claims that she accepted voluntary departure. The order granting voluntary departure indicates that appeal was waived.
 
The respondent filed an appeal on June 16, 2006, claiming ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent also filed a motion to reopen and remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). See Hamid v. Ashcroft, 336 F.3d 465, 469 (6th Cir. 2003).
 
In addition, by joint motion filed August 22, 2006, current counsel for the respondent and Chief Counsel of the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) request a remand for evidentiary hearing on the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. If the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel, her waiver may not have been knowing and intelligent so we would have jurisdiction to reopen and remand. See Matter of Patino, 23 I&N Dec. 74 (BIA 2001). Further, a joint motion to reopen is not subject to the time and number limitations of a motion to reopen whether filed with the Board or the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(iii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(iv).
 
In light of the joint motion, as well as the respondent’s compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, we find that the Immigration Judge should consider the respondent’s motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel before the Board resolves the appeal in this case. On remand, the parties should present their request for a subpoena to the Immigration Judge. The record will therefore be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
*2 ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for the reasons stated in this decision.

FOR THE BOARD
 
2006 WL 3485802 (BIA)

2006 WL 3485801 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: USMAN MALIK MANSOOR
FILE: AXX XX9 915 - DALLAS
November 7, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Husein A. Abdelhadi, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on May 11, 2005, when we denied the respondent’s March 30, 2005, motion to reopen. On October 4, 2006, the respondent filed the instant motion in which he asks us to reopen these proceedings on our own motion and to reissue our March 14, 2005, decision so that he can file a petition for review with the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit. For the reasons stated below, the motion will be denied.
 
First, we consider the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied as time and number barred. See sections 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) (an alien may file one motion to reopen and, with certain exceptions not relevant here, it shall be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order of removal). The final administrative decision in this case was issued on March 14, 2005. This is the respondent’s second motion to reopen.
 
The respondent asks us to reopen these proceedings on our own motion based on his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997)(holding that the Board’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship). The respondent alleges that his prior attorney, Mr. Anthony Osei, never informed him that he could file a petition for review of our March 14, 2005, order in federal court. The respondent also alleges that because Mr. Osei failed to timely file a writ of habeas corpus in federal court after the issuance of our May 11, 2005, decision, he lost his opportunity to seek judicial review of our order. The respondent complied with the procedural requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
On October 18, 2006, we received Mr. Osei’s response to the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Mr. Osei adamantly denies the respondent’s allegations of misconduct. First, Mr. Osei explains that the respondent was properly informed of his options after this Board dismissed his appeal. According to the fee receipts, the respondent paid $600 in legal fees for a motion to reopen and he paid the $110 filing fees. Compare Mr. Osei’s Affidavit and Attachments F and G with Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 7-8, and Attachments A and B. Second, Mr. Osei emphatically denies that he ever directed, prepared or filed a writ of habeas corpus on behalf of the respondent. He also asserts that he was never paid to prepare such a filing. Compare Mr. Osei’s Affidavit at 4 with Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 8 and Attachments A, B and E. Based on the foregoing, we find that the respondent has failed to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel that would support sua sponte reopening.
 
*2 The respondent has also asked us to reissue our March 14, 2005, decision so he can seek review in federal court. The respondent has failed to show that a reissued decision is warranted in this case. Notably, the respondent does not allege that Mr. Osei failed to notify him of our decision, or that Board did not properly mail the decision to Mr. Osei, or that Mr. Osei did not receive the Board’s decision. Rather, the respondent argues that given his counsel’s ineffective assistance, this is the only way to properly remedy the case. See Respondent’s Motion at 13. As noted above, on the basis of the record before us, we cannot find that Mr. Osei’s assistance was ineffective. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reissue will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.

2006 WL 3485761 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PERRY N. GUMBS
FILE: AXX XX5 583 - FISHKILL
November 7, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Laura A. Michalec
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
  
APPLICATION: Termination
 
The respondent moves to reopen our October 27, 2005, decision summarily, dismissing his appeal. The Department of Homeland Security, (DHS) opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed not later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on January 25, 2006, since the final administrative decision was issued on October 27, 2005. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time. The respondent contends that his criminal conviction was not final; however, the DHS points out that the documentation provided by the respondent does not establish that his conviction is on direct appeal. The DHS observes that the respondent has instead filed a motion for post-conviction relief. The mere availability of post-conviction motions or other forms of collateral attack does not affect the finality of the conviction. See Matter of Ponce de Leon, 21 I&N Dec. 154, 157 (BIA 1996). See generally Matter of Polanco, 20 I&N Dec. 894, 896 (BIA 1994) (concluding that a conviction is final when the right to direct appeal is exhausted); see also Marino v. INS, 537 F.2d 686, 691-92 (2nd Cir. 1976) (explaining that an alien is not deemed to have been “convicted” of a crime under the Immigration and Nationality Act until the conviction has attained a substantial degree of finality, and such finality does not occur unless and until direct appellate review of the conviction, as contrasted with collateral attack, has been exhausted or waived). We cannot entertain a collateral attack on a judgment of conviction, unless that judgment is void on its face, and cannot go behind the judicial record to determine the guilt or innocence of an alien. Matter of Rodriguez-Carrillo, 22 I&N Dec. 1031, 1034 (BIA 1999).
 
The respondent also alleges ineffective assistance of counsel, but has not established that the deadline for submitting a motion to reopen should be equitably tolled. See Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167, 170 (2d Cir.2006) (citing Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 128 (2d Cir.2000)). The court stated in Cekic, “Tolling is available if the alien can demonstrate that (1) his counsel’s conduct violated ¿hisº constitutional right to due process, and (2) the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period ¿ heº seeks to toll.” Id. To make this showing, an alien must demonstrate that he pursued his case “¿fºrom the point at which the ineffective assistance of counsel should have been, or was in fact, discovered.” See id. at 171 (citing Iavorski, 232 F.3d at 135). It is the respondent’s burden to establish that the 90-day deadline should be equitably tolled, but he has not established due diligence. Moreover, the respondent has manifestly failed to comply with any of the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (B1A), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), wherein we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. See Jian Yun Zheng v. U.S. Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 46-47 (2d Cir.2005). Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Review Granted in Part, Cause Remanded by Huezo-Landaverde v. Mukasey, 9th Cir., December 28, 2007
2006 WL 3485779 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIO ERNESTO HUEZO-LANDAVERDE
FILE: AXX XX8 060 - LOS ANGELES
October 31, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Patricia Duggan, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered removed from the United States because he failed to appear for his hearing scheduled for April 26, 2000. The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on June 5, 2006, but the Immigration Judge denied it on July 28, 2006. This appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent does not dispute on appeal that he received the Notice to Appear. He indicates in his affidavit that he moved to Seattle in 1999, mailed a change of address form to the Immigration Court, and was even told by his aunt that a Notice of Hearing was not received on his behalf. He argues that he complied with his obligation to notify the Immigration Court of his address change, and these proceedings should be reopened.
 
We have reviewed the record, and note, as did the Immigration Judge, that a change of address form is not contained therein. We further note that the respondent did not submit a statement from his aunt to corroborate that the respondent lived with her at 1235 S. Sharon Court, #K3, Anaheim, CA 92804, and the respondent’s Notice of Hearing was not received by her at that address. We therefore do not find that the respondent rebutted the presumption that the Notice of Hearing was properly delivered at his last known address. Salta v. INS, 314 F.3d 1076 (9th Cir. 2002). We further cannot find that the respondent proved that he mailed a change of address form to the Immigration Court immediately after moving to Seattle. We note that the respondent does not indicate in his affidavit the address that he allegedly moved to, and when he moved. We therefore cannot find based on the general representation reflected in the affidavit that the respondent timely notified the Immigration Court of his address change.
 
The respondent further argues on appeal that he was ineffectively represented by his former counsel in these proceedings. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639, we held that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the claim reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. In addition, in Reyes v. Ashcroft,, 348 F.3d 1126 (9th Cir. 2003), the court noted that the alien failed to give the prior counsel notice of his motion to reopen. Id. at 1131-1132. The court emphasized that the purpose of Lozada’s notice requirement is to afford the prior counsel an opportunity to contest the allegations of ineffective assistance so that the adjudicator can more accurately assess the claim of ineffective assistance. Id. at 1132.
 
*2 In the instant case, the respondent has failed to submit a complaint against his former attorney with the proper authority, and did not notify him of the allegation of negligence. No explanation for the failure to do so was provided. We further do not find that a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel is present. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9 th Cir. 2000). We cannot find that prior counsel was in any way responsible for the respondent’s failure to appear for the hearing, or materially impeded his right to pursue this appeal. We find that it is appropriate to require compliance with the above requirements. The respondent has not done so, and his request for remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel is denied.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD
 
2006 WL 3485779 (BIA)
End of Document

2006 WL 3485783 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SANKAR KUMAR SAHA
FILE: AXX XX1 481 - OAKDALE
October 31, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Dominick S. Cardinale, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Craig A. Harlow,
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 In an order entered on August 2, 2006, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal by affirming the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent, through different counsel, has filed a motion to reopen, based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen shall not be granted unless it appears that the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 3.2(c)(1).
 
The respondent argues that his former counsel “failed to properly articulate” the basis for eligibility for a waiver of inadmissibility, that he failed to “make any effort to review or reopen the facts” of his criminal case, and that he failed “to properly review and discuss alternative applications for relief.”
 
Litigants are generally bound by the conduct of their attorneys absent egregious circumstances. LeBlanc v. INS, 715 F.2d 685 (1st Cir. 1983): Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Ineffective assistance of counsel is a denial of due process only if the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his or her case. Mejia Rodriguez v. INS, 178 F.3d 1139 (11th Cir. 1999); Ramirez-Durazo v. INS, 794 F.2d 491 (9 th Cir. 1986).
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must be supported by: 1) an affidavit by the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; 2) a showing that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and 3) a showing that a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, or if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, supra. See also Stroe v. INS, 256 F.3d 498 (7th Cir. 2001). The respondent has failed to show that he has complied with the last of these requirements, and so his motion must be denied.
 
Moreover, given the respondent’s circumstances, he is ineligible for a waiver of inadmissibility pursuant to the former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), it is difficult to conceive of an argument that former counsel could have made before the Immigration Judge that would have somehow rendered him eligible for that relief. Neither the Immigration Judge nor this Board is imbued with the authority to “review or reopen the facts” of the respondent’s criminal proceedings, in terms of addressing guilt or innocence, or attacking the conviction. Former counsel’s participation in the respondent’s criminal proceedings is a matter beyond the scope of our jurisdiction.
 
*2 We are aware of no relief from removal for which the respondent would be eligible, so it is unclear how former counsel could have rendered ineffective assistance by not discussing alternative relief. The respondent obviously harbored no fear of harm in Bangladesh, having traveled to that country in 2002 to get married, and again in 2004 for the birth of his child. Given the respondent’s frequent travels to Bangladesh, and the lack of any mention at the hearings about any fear of harm, the Immigration Judge was under no obligation to discuss withholding of removal or deferral of removal under the Convention Against Torture.
 
We note that current counsel has represented the respondent at least since April 2006, and counsel submitted an extensive appellate brief on the respondent’s behalf, regarding eligibility for section 212(c) relief. However, no mention was made by either current counsel or the respondent prior to our order of any alleged ineffective assistance. It is unclear why current counsel and the respondent would have waited until after the Board rendered its order to present allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. In any event, those allegations are unavailing.
 
We find no indication of prejudice to the respondent’s case by the quality of representation rendered by his former counsel.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2006 WL 3485783 (BIA)
End of Document

2006 WL 3485789 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANTHONY E. ADEFOPE
FILE: AXX XX6 943 - HOUSTON
October 31, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tracy C. Udenwagu, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rory H. Potter
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This matter was last before the Board on July 20, 2001, when we issued an order affirming the Immigration Judge’s April 5, 1999, decision finding the respondent removable as charged, determining that good cause for a continuance of his proceedings was not demonstrated inasmuch as there was no showing that the immediate relative spousal visa petition a filed in his behalf had been approved, and ordering him removed to his native Nigeria. On March 2, 2006, the respondent filed a motion with this Board seeking to reopen his proceedings so that he may be pursue adjustment of status given that his United States citizen wife has filed a new visa petition in his behalf. He also asserts that he was not effectively represented by counsel in his prior proceedings.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely by 4 1/2 years and therefore is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (2006) (providing that, with certain exceptions not presented here, a motion to reopen must be filed with the Board within 90 days after the mailing of the Board decision). The Board retains limited discretionary powers under the regulations to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte in unique situations where it would serve the interests of justice. However, we have considered the arguments set forth in the respondent’s motion in light of the record before us and have concluded that such reopening is not justified here. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a) (2006); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
The respondent is not currently eligible for adjustment of status. There has been no showing that the visa petition filed February 24, 2006, by his current United States citizen wife, Anthonia Ngozi Monogo, has been approved. This is the second petition filed by Ms. Monogo in the respondent’s behalf. The first petition filed by her on September 30, 1997, was denied by the district director on May 12, 2003,1 pursuant to section 204(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act given a prior determination by the district director that the respondent’s marriage to his first United States, citizen wife, Reba Adefope, was a sham. See district director decision, May 15, 1996 (denying the visa petition filed by Reba Adefope); Board order, June 6, 1997 (dismissing the petitioner’s appeal of this decision). Both of the district director’s decisions are now final.
 
Further, notwithstanding the respondent’s assertions to the contrary, the circumstances presented here do not fall within the purview of our holding in Matter of Velarde-Pacheco, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002). The instant motion to reopen is untimely and therefore is not properly filed. In addition, the DHS opposes the motion on substantive grounds, and not only because the underlying visa petition has not yet been adjudicated.
 
*2 The respondent also asserts that he was denied effective assistance of counsel in his prior proceedings. This claim has not been adequately developed or explained by the respondent. See Respondent’s Motion, p. 2. In any event, he has failed to comply with any of the requirements discussed in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for advancing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See generally Lara v. Troiminski, 216 F.3d 487, 498 (5th Cir. 2000).
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

1

In an order dated October 20, 2006, we dismissed the appeal of this decision as untimely.
