2006 WL 3485611 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GLEN BRIAN CAMPBELL
FILE: AXX XX5 529 - HOUSTON
October 17, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sharlene S. Richards, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Gerrie Zhang
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on June 14, 2005, when we affirmed, without opinion, the Immigration Judge’s April 14, 2004, decision finding the respondent inadmissible as charged and ineligible for relief from removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(4).
 
On June 15, 2006, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen these proceedings to afford him the opportunity to apply for voluntary departure under section section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a). The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the respondent’s motion because it is untimely filed and the respondent is ineligible for voluntary departure under section 240B(a) if the Act because he is an arriving alien.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied as untimely filed. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i) (with certain exceptions not relevant here, a motion to reopen shall be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order of removal).
 
The respondent argues that the motions deadline should be equitably tolled based on his claim of ineffective assistance by his former counsel, Ms. Martha E. Garza. The United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, the jurisdiction wherein this case arises, has not published an opinion explicitly adopting the doctrine of equitable tolling in the context of a motion to reopen immigration proceedings. However, even if we assume that such tolling is available to the respondent, he has not established that he is entitled to it based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Although the respondent has substantially complied with the procedural steps set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), he has failed to prove any prejudice resulting from his former counsel’s performance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
First, the respondent asserts that because Ms. Garza failed to file a timely appeal brief with this Board that he will never know whether he “had a meritorious appeal.” See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Exhibit 12 (Respondent’s Affidavit at 3). This Board could have, but did not, summarily dismiss the respondent’s appeal pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(E) (an appeal may be summarily dismissed where a party indicates on Form EOIR-26 that he will file a brief or statement in support of the appeal and, thereafter, does not file such a brief or statement, or reasonably explain his failure to do so, within the time set for filing). Instead, we summarily affirmed with decision of the Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(4). The respondent has failed to show that the filing of an appeal brief would have altered the decision in his case.
 
*2 Second, the respondent asserts that he was prejudiced by the Immigration Judge’s failure to inform him of his right to apply for pre-hearing voluntary departure. Arriving aliens, like the respondent, are barred from applying for voluntary departure in lieu of being placed in proceedings or prior to the completion of such proceedings under section 240B(a)(1) of the Act. See section 240B(a)(4) of the Act.
 
Third, the respondent asserts that there could have been a change in the law which might have affected his appeal. The respondent’s assertion is speculative and, in fact, never came to pass. In sum, the respondent has failed to establish how his case was prejudiced as a result of Ms. Garza’s alleged misconduct.
 
Finally, inasmuch as the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge abused her discretion when she failed to grant him another continuance to afford him the opportunity to identify a form of relief for which he was eligible, he has yet to identify a form of relief from removal for which he is eligible. Based on the foregoing, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2006 WL 3485611 (BIA)
End of Document

2006 WL 3485699 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ERROL ANTHONY WALKER
FILE: AXX XX0 339 - HARTFORD
October 16, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gregory Osakwe, Esquire
*1 On April 28, 1998, this Board summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent has filed a motion to reopen in August 2006, claiming that he was a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion will be denied.
 
The appeal was summarily dismissed because of the failure to file a separate brief or statement. The appeal form itself contained allegations relating to the respondent’s criminal proceedings and failed to raise an appellate issue that we could address. The respondent claims in his motion that his counsel in his criminal proceedings provided ineffective assistance, as did his counsel in his deportation proceedings. Allegedly, the respondent’s former counsel in his deportation proceedings, who reportedly cannot be located, was suspended from practicing law in Connecticut in April 2006, and he filed a complaint against her on August 21, 2006.
 
The nature and quality of the respondent’s representation in his criminal proceedings involves a matter beyond our jurisdiction to address.
 
The respondent does not explain why he waited over 8 years to file a motion to reopen or, for that matter, to file a complaint against his former counsel with her state bar disciplinary board. The failure to file a brief may amount to deprivation of due process. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). However, we find no basis here for an exception to the time limitations on motions to reopen, in view of the respondent’s failure to exercise due diligence on his own behalf.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: JORGE FUENTES-GUTIERREZ
FILE: AXX XX3 663 - LOS ANGELES
October 16, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Victor D. Nieblas Pradis, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on April 29, 2003. In that decision, we upheld an Immigration Judge’s March 13, 2002, decision, in which she declined to reconsider her prior decision denying suspension of deportation. We denied a motion to reopen on February 26, 2004, as untimely. We denied another motion to reopen as late, and number-barred, on May 1, 2006. The pending motion was filed late, on May 26, 2006, and is barred by numerical limitations for motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion, which will be granted.
 
The respondent’s previously filed motions sought to have proceedings reopened in order to pursue an application for adjustment of status, based on marriage to a United States citizen. See section 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1245; Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002). An I-130 immediate relative visa petition was approved on the respondent’s behalf on August 8, 2003.
 
The respondent, in his previous motions to reopen, blamed attorney Timothy McCandless, who has been suspended from practice before the Board, for providing ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent now argues that attorney Walter Burrier, who has also been suspended from practice before the Board, committed ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent argues that Burrier sent a substitute attorney to the immigration court to represent the respondent concerning his suspension claim. The respondent states that, after the Immigration Judge denied his motion to reconsider, and this Board affirmed that denial, Burrier agreed to file a petition for review with the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals. The respondent contends that Burrier then assigned McCandless to file the petition for review, without notifying the respondent, but McCandless failed to file the petition for review. Burrier then filed the first motion to reopen with the Board, which was denied as untimely. The respondent states that Burrier assured him that he would take care of everything. The respondent states that Burrier then prepared the second motion to reopen, which was presented as a pro se motion during the time that Burrier was suspended from practice before the Board. The motion was denied as untimely on May 1, 2006. After the second motion was denied, the respondent consulted current counsel and the pending motion was filed shortly thereafter.
 
*2 The respondent argues that Burrier failed to assist him in timely presenting evidence concerning his application for suspension of deportation, and prepared ineffective motions to reopen (Respondent’s Mot., at p. 15). The respondent has complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Under the circumstances, we agree that the motions deadline should be excused.
 
Accordingly, the record is remanded in order to afford the respondent an opportunity to present his application for adjustment of status to the Immigration Judge, and to provide further evidence concerning his application for suspension of deportation.

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Remanded June 1, 2007
2006 WL 3203627 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FERNANDO JOSE RIVERA-RIVERA
FILE: A46 948 686 - NEWARK
October 3, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joseph C. Sekula, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U-S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on December 22, 2004, at which time we summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 29, 2003. The respondent now seeks the reopening of his immigration proceedings based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded to the respondent’s motion. The motion will be denied.
 
In Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), this Board discussed the evidence that must be presented in order for an alien to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel arising in immigration proceedings. See also Zheng v. Gonzales, 422 F.3d 98, 106 (3d Cir. 2005); Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127, 131-35 (3d Cir. 2001); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556, 561-62 (BIA 2003). See generally Filja v. Gonzales, 447 F.3d 241, 256 (3d Cir. 2006); Luntungan v. Att’y Gen. of the United States, 449 F.3d 551 (3d Cir. 2006). In that opinion, we held that not only must an alien satisfy certain procedural requirements, but he must also demonstrate that his attorney’s actions caused him actual prejudice — for instance, the alien must show that but for the errors complained of there was a reasonable probability that he would not have been ordered removed. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640; see also Ponce-Leiva v. Ashcroft, 331 F.3d 369, 377-78 (3d Cir. 2003). In the present matter, the respondent has not complied with all of the evidentiary requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada. See Motion to Reopen, sub-exhs. E-F. Specifically, the respondent has failed to submit an affidavit setting forth in detail his attorney-client agreement. Without evidence of the respondent’s agreement with his former attorney, we cannot conclude that there was ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Furthermore, even were we to assume that his prior counsel had been defective, the instant motion must still be denied because the respondent has not shown that he suffered any prejudice as a result of his attorney’s conduct. For instance, in his motion, the respondent principally argues that his case should be reopened in order to provide him with an opportunity to request the “humanitarian reinstatement” of the visa petition that his mother, who is deceased, previously submitted on his behalf.1 See Exh. 2A at 2 (stating that his mother passed away on May 16, 1995); cf. section 205 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1155; 8 C.F.R. § 1205.1(a)(3)(i)(C) (requiring the revocation of a family-sponsored petition upon the death of the petitioner “unless the Attorney General in his or her discretion determines that for humanitarian reasons revocation would be inappropriate”); see also sections 2(a)(1) and 2(b), Family Sponsor Immigration Act of 2002, Pub. L. No. 107-150, 116 Stat. 74 (codified as amended at section 213A(f)(5) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1183a(f)(5)). See generally INS Memorandum for Regional Directors - Policy Change - Public Law 107-150, the Family Sponsor Immigration Act of 2002: Use of Substitute Sponsor if Visa Petitioner Has Died (HQADJ 70/21.1.1) (June 15, 2002) (hereinafter “INS Memorandum”), available at http:// www.uscis.gov/graphics/lawsregs/handbook/PL107_150Pub.pdf. However, the reinstatement of the respondent’s visa petition would not by itself provide the respondent with any relief from removal. See section 213A(f)(B) of the Act; see also section 212(a)(4)(C)(ii) of the Act. To the contrary, he would still need to establish his eligibility for either an immigrant visa or section 245 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255. See INS Memorandum at 2. For reasons that will be discussed below, we find that the respondent has failed to show that he is prima facie eligible for either form of relief.
 
*2 To establish his eligibility to either adjust his status or obtain an immigrant visa abroad, the respondent must demonstrate that he is admissible to the United States for permanent residence. See, e.g., section 245(a) of the Act. See generally Zheng v. Gonzales, 422 F.3d 98 (3d Cir. 2005). In the present matter, the respondent’s NTA charged him with two grounds of inadmissibility, both of which were sustained by the Immigration Judge.2 See Exh. 1. Although the respondent argues that his prior counsel prejudiced his case by failing to demonstrate that he is inadmissible under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that he is removable as charged.3 See Interlocutory IJ Dec. (Sept. 15, 2003). See generally Motion to Reopen at 3. The validity of the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent is removable as charged is particularly important here because before the respondent could obtain either a new immigrant visa or adjust his status, he must demonstrate his ability to waive any and all applicable grounds of inadmissibility. See generally section 212 of the Act. Unfortunately, the respondent has not submitted any waiver applications. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) (“A motion to reopen proceedings for the purpose of submitting an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documentation.”); see also Filja v. Gonzales, supra, at 255 (discussing the “three principal grounds” on which the Board may deny a motion to reopen immigration proceedings). Moreover, he has failed to make out a prima facie case for any such applications, all of which are discretionary.4 Accordingly, we find that the respondent has not shown that he suffered any prejudice as a result of his prior attorney’s conduct.
 
In view of the foregoing, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s request for a stay of removal is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

With regard to the sufficiency of the respondent’s Notice to Appear (“NTA”), we note that the Act and the regulations do not require that an immigration official’s signature or title be included on a Notice to Appear. See section 239(a)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.15(c). See generally 8 C.F.R. §§ 239(a), 1239.1(a). Furthermore, the regulations state that the omission of any of the enumerated items “shall not provide the alien with any substantive or procedural rights.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.15(c). Therefore, we do not find that the respondent’s Notice to Appear (NTA) is defective for failing to comply with section 239 of the Act or the regulations.

2

The respondent does not contest the Immigration Judge’s finding that he is inadmissible pursuant to section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) of the Act.

3

During the earlier proceedings, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent was removable pursuant to section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act as a result of his willful misrepresentation of the fact that his mother had passed away before the issuance of his immigrant visa. See Interlocutory IJ Dec. (Sept. 15, 2003). See generally 8 C.F.R. § 1205.1(a)(3)(i)(C). The respondent has never testified or submitted any evidence indicating that he did not know that his immigrant visa application at least insinuated that his mother was still alive at the time he obtained his visa. See Exh. 2E (indicating, in 1998, that the respondent’s father was deceased; but providing a current United States address for his mother); cf. Exh. 2A at 2. See generally Exhs. 2B, 2F and 2G. Inasmuch as his failure to provide this information cut off a line of inquiry on an issue that was material to his ability to procure an immigrant visa, we agree that he has failed to demonstrate that he is not removable under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act.

4

The respondent has failed to even establish that he is prima facie eligible for the humanitarian reinstatement of the visa petition that his mother filed on his behalf. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2; see also Filja v. Gonzales, supra, at 255. While the Family Sponsor Immigration Act of 2002 would provide him with a practical means of seeking reinstatement, to do so he would still need to submit an affidavit of support (Form I-864) from a “substitute” sponsor. Insofar as the statute permits only certain family members to qualify as such and the respondent has not shown that he has a relative who is both willing to act as his substitute sponsor and could qualify as one, he has not submitted sufficient evidence from which we could infer that he has even a plausible claim for relief. Moreover, in light of the manner in which the respondent previously obtained an immigrant visa, we likewise conclude that he has not shown that he would merit this, or any, discretionary grant form of relief.

2006 WL 3088874 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MELENDEZ-DE SORTO, PAULINA
FILE: AXX-XX9-556 - HARLINGEN
September 29, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Schwarz, S. Bernard, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mark R. Whitworth,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 05, 2006. The respondent’s attorney appeared at the respondent’s hearing on October 27, 1986, but the respondent failed to appear. The respondent was ordered to be deported in proceedings conducted in absentia. More than 17 years later, the respondent filed two motions to reopen, claiming that she had no notice of the hearing and that she is eligible for relief from deportation. The Immigration Judge found that the notice was properly served on the respondent’s counsel who had appeared at the hearing, and who attested that he sent notice to the respondent but she did not maintain contact with him. He denied both motions. On October 20, 2005, the respondent filed a third motion to reopen. She claimed ineffective assistance of counsel. The Immigration Judge found that he could not determine what agreement the respondent had with her former counsel, and that there was no evidence that she had informed him of her averments: He concluded that she had not complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), affd, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), and that her motion was untimely. On appeal, the respondent correctly points out that her motion included her statement of the agreement she had with her former counsel, notice to him of her allegations, and his response. See Motion to Reopen dated October 14, 2005, Exhibits G and H-1. We agree that she has complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra. However, the evidence indicates that the respondent’s former counsel sent the hearing notice to the respondent at the address she provided, and filed the change of venue motion that she requested. See Exhibit H-1, supra. He appeared at the hearing and explained that the respondent failed to maintain contact with him. In fact, the respondent failed to contact her former counsel or inquire into the status of her deportation proceedings for more than 17 years. The evidence does not demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent’s lack of diligence indicates that favorable discretion is not warranted. The motion was appropriately denied. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2006 WL 3088735 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VACHE IBRAHIM NERCESSIAN A.K.A. PHILIP HADADEEN A.K.A. HAIG R. ALLEN
FILE: AXX XX1 221 - EL PASO
September 27, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carrye Washington, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on March 29, 2002, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s applications for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), and adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255. On June 29, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to reopen in which he claims that he received ineffective assistance from his former criminal attorney, Karen Stevens, and the appellate attorney in his criminal case, Daniel Koryn, as well as from his former immigration attorney, Steve Hershberger, whom he claims did not raise an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against the two criminal/appellate attorneys and who did not raise sufficiently aggressive arguments regarding the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of a section 212(h) waiver. Through his motion, the respondent seeks to again apply for a section 212(h) waiver of inadmissibility and adjustment of status. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed no response to the motion. The respondent’s motion for a stay of removal was denied on July 20, 2006. The motion to reopen is denied.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely because it was not filed within 90 days of the Board’s decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). In addition, the respondent’s motion does not comply with any of the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Statements of counsel regarding whether bar complaints were filed or former counsels notified of the allegations are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). As the respondent has not complied with the Lozada requirements, his motion to reopen is denied. See Lara v. Trominiski, 216 F.3d 487, 498 (5th Cir. 2000) (holding that the general application of the Lozada requirements does not constitute an abuse of discretion by the Board). See also Gutierrez-Morales v. Homan, ___ F.3d ___, 2006 WL 2390283 at 1, n. 4 (5th Cir. Aug. 21, 2006) (stating that alien had failed to comply with Lozada requirements and the application of those requirements was not an abuse of discretion).
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.

2006 WL 3088705 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SANTIAGO HERNANDEZ SALDANA A.K.A. LUIS SALDANA-SARDIN
FILE: AXX XX0 794 - LOS ANGELES
September 22, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard Camarena, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. In a decision dated May 8, 2006, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen his removal proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia under section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A). The respondent filed a timely appeal from that decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that notice to the respondent’s counsel constitutes notice to the respondent. See Section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255. The record reflects that the Notice of Hearing was sent to the respondent’s counsel by regular mail (Exh. 3) and included the date and time of his hearing in October 2005. The respondent did not appear, at the hearing and the Immigration Judge correctly ordered him removed in absentia.
 
Written notice is considered sufficient if provided at the most recent address provided under section 239(a)(1)(F)of the Act. Contrary to the respondent’s argument on appeal, the record reflects that the Notice of Hearing was mailed to the respondent’s counsel at his correct address, as reflected in the Form EOIR-28. There is no indication that the hearing notice was returned to the Immigration Court.
 
Furthermore, this case is distinguishable from Salta v. INS, 314 F.3d 1076 (9th Cir. 2002), wherein the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals determined that, under the circumstances, a sworn affidavit from the alien that neither she nor a responsible party residing at her address received the notice was sufficient to rebut the presumption of delivery and entitle an alien to an evidentiary hearing to consider the veracity of her allegations. In that case, the alien had actually initiated a proceeding to obtain a benefit and an affidavit was submitted with the motion, Id. at 1079.
 
In the instant matter, there is no indication that the respondent initiated removal proceedings. Moreover, the respondent did not submit an affidavit to overcome the presumption of proper delivery.
 
Lastly, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel fails because the respondent has not complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aft’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In that case, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned is informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. The Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, where this case arises, has found the application of the noted requirements to generally be reasonable, but has held that “they need not be rigidly enforced where their purpose is fully served by other means.” Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000). Here, however, the respondent has failed to even minimally fulfill any of the above-noted requirements. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2006 WL 3088705 (BIA)
End of Document

2006 WL 3088682 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JESUS GARCIA-MORALES
FILE: AXX XX3 374 - IMPERIAL
September 22, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jeff Lindblad
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N ACt ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Section 212(c) waiver
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s February 24, 2006, decision denying him a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (the “Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (repealed 1996).1 The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The issues on appeal include whether a fair hearing was conducted before the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s application for a section 212(c) waiver. The respondent contends on appeal that the Immigration Judge abused his discretion and violated the respondent’s due process, both by admitting into evidence proffered by the respondent’s spouse without requiring her to testify, and by going outside the record of conviction in denying his application. He also argues that, as the counsel who represented him both before the Immigration Judge, failed to have the Immigration Judge suppress the evidence of the restraining order, he received ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent argues that, by going outside the record of conviction in denying his waiver application, there was a denial of due process. However, the Immigration Judge, upon review of the record as a whole, balances the adverse factors evidencing an alien’s undesirability as a permanent resident with the social and humane considerations presented in his behalf to determine whether the granting of section 212(c) relief appears in the best interests of this country. See Matter of Marin, 16 I&N Dec. 581, 585 (BIA 1978). The respondent has questioned the admissibility of a restraining order, arguing that the order was entered in absentia, as well as the admissibility of a letter from his spouse (Gp. Exh. 9). While the respondent challenges the validity of the order, he has conceded that he was aware of the court date, but failed to appear (Tr. at 114). An Immigration Judge may receive into evidence any oral or written statement which is material and relevant to any issue in the case made by the alien or any other person during any investigation or examination. Such evidence may be admitted as long as it is probative and its use is fundamentally fair so as not to violate due process standards. Matter of Escobar, 18 I&N Dec. 412 (BIA 1983); Matter of Exilus, 18 I&N Dec. 276 (BIA 1982). As for the statement from his spouse, his counsel objected to its use as there was no opportunity to cross-examine her (Tr. at 102-3). The Immigration Judge acknowledged that problem (Tr. at 104), and we note that the Immigration Judge considered the restraining order but gave no weight to his spouse’s statement, making no reference to the contents of the letter in his decision. To the extent that the respondent argues that prior counsel’s representation was ineffective, he has not substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and has not provided persuasive evidence of prejudice arising from counsel’s allegedly deficient performance, i.e, evidence reflecting a reasonable possibility that the outcome of his removal hearing would have been different had counsel objected to clearly admissible evidence, the restraining order. See Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999) (finding that prejudice must be shown for a due process challenge and that prejudice is found when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome). Accordingly, there has been no showing that the respondent’s hearing was not fairly conducted.
 
*2 As for the adequacy of the Immigration Judge’s decision, the respondent argues that the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) failed to prove that he is undeserving of a waiver. However, regulations provide that the respondent, not the DHS, shall have the burden of establishing that he is eligible for any requested benefit or privilege and that it should be granted in the exercise of discretion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d). The Immigration Judge properly weighed all of the adverse and favorable factors in denying the respondent a section 212(c) waiver. Matter of Marin, supra. The Immigration Judge considered all of those favorable factors, individually and cumulatively, but nonetheless concluded, based upon the adverse factors of record, including the crimes the respondent had committed, that this outweighed the favorable factors. No arguments have been presented on appeal by the respondent which persuade us that the Immigration Judge’s decision should be disturbed. In sum, we find that the respondent’s application for a section 212(c) waiver was properly denied by the Immigration Judge.
 
The respondent finally argues in his appeal that the proceedings were an unconstitutional violation of equal protection and due process. It is well-settled that this Board has no jurisdiction to adjudicate facial constitutional challenges to the Act or its implementing regulations. Matter of C-20 I&N Dec. 529, 532 (BIA 1992).
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

We note that, while the respondent argued on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in finding him removable for having been convicted of an aggravated felony, he was not charged with being removable on this basis.

2006 WL 3088698 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE LEONEL BAEZ FERNANDEZ
FILE: AXX XX0 875 - NEW YORK CITY
September 21, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ruchi Thaker, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This matter was last before the Board on March 24, 2006, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s November 22, 2004, order finding the respondent removable as charged and denying his applications for relief from removal, in particular his application for relief under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, (the “Act”) 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c). On June 22, 2006, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen the proceedings, asserting that his former counsel provided him ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to call attention to the case Restrepo v. McElroy, 369 F.3d 627 (2d Cir. 2004). The motion will be denied.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-640 (BIA 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counser’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. The respondent’s motion satisfies each of these elements.
 
However, Lozada, also requires the claimant to establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 561-562 (BIA 2003). The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 311 (BIA 1998). For the reasons stated below, we find that the representation was not negligent and the respondent has not shown prejudice in this case.
 
The sole basis for the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim is that former counsel, the Immigration Judge, and this Board failed to consider Restrepo. According to the respondent, Restrepo stands for the proposition that an alien who relied on the availability of § 212(c) relief in going to trial, rather than pleading guilty, remains eligible for a § 212(c) waiver after the repeal of the statute. We disagree. Under governing Second Circuit precedent, the repeal of § 212(c) is not impermissibly retroactive as applied to aliens like the respondent who were convicted of a criminal charge at trial before the elimination of § 212(c), because they “cannot show that they altered their conduct in reliance on the availability of such relief.” Rankine v. Reno, 319 F.3d 93, 100 (2d Cir. 2003) (emphasis added); cf. INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289, 326 (holding that “§ 212(c) relief remains available for aliens … whose convictions were obtained through plea agreements and who, notwithstanding those convictions, would have been eligible for § 212(c) relief at the time of their plea under the law then in effect”). In Thorn v. Ashcroft, 369 F.3d 158, 161-63 (2d Cir. 2004), the Second Circuit reaffirmed Rankine, and distinguished Restrepo, as a case in which the alien claimed an alternative basis for relying on the availability of § 212(c). Specifically, the petitioner in Restrepo claimed that he decided to forgo the opportunity to apply for § 212(c) relief “affirmatively” under 8 C.F.R. § 212.3 (b), before being placed in deportation proceedings. Restrepo, at 622, 636.
 
*2 Unlike the petitioner in Restrepo, the respondent has not raised an alternative basis for relying on the availability of § 212(c) relief. In particular, he has made no assertion that he delayed filing an affirmative application for § 212(c) relief in reliance upon the continued existence of § 212(c).1 Therefore, we find that the respondent’s case falls squarely within the holding of Rankine, as an alien convicted of a criminal charge at trial and that he suffered no prejudice through his former counsel’s failure to cite Restrepo.2 In light of our finding that Restrepo is not controlling, we need not address the respondent’s remaining arguments, each of which stem from that case. Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

We further note that a petitioner claiming reliance based on the Restrepo set of circumstances—where an alien claims detrimental reliance on the continued existence § 212(c) in delaying the filing of an affirmative application for § 212(c) relief—must make an individualized showing of reliance on the continued existence of § 212(c) relief. Thompson v. Ridge, 2005 WL 433277 (S.D.N.Y. 2005) (noting that the Second Circuit in Restrepo left open the question of whether individual reliance must be shown or a “categorical” approach should be applied to all such aliens). The respondent has made no such showing in this case.

2

Additionally, we note that former counsel argued on appeal that the respondent was eligible for § 212(c) relief under Ponnapula v. Ashcroft, 373 F.3d 480 (3d Cir. 2004), a case in which the petitioner was permitted to file a § 212(c) application even though he did not accept a plea agreement and was convicted after trial. Although a Third Circuit decision is not binding in this case, we find that the citation of Ponnapula was sufficient to call attention to the respondent’s potential eligibility for § 212(c) relief under the St. Cyr, supra, line of cases.

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Case Reopened by IN RE: ADONAY REMBERTO ORTIZ, BIA, April 21, 2011
2006 WL 3088919 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ORTIZ, ADONAY REMBERTO
FILE: AXX-XX7-988 - LOS ANGELES
September 19, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Vargas, Patricia, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 11, 2006. The respondent’s case was last before us on January 4, 2002, when we remanded the record to the Immigration Judge to provide the respondent with an opportunity to seek a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act pursuant to INS v. St. Cyr, 121 S. Ct. 2271 (2001). The record reflects that the respondent was present with counsel on October 13, 2005, when the Immigration Judge provided notice concerning the next hearing to be held on January 19, 2006. On January 19, 2006, the respondent failed to appear. On March 28, 2006, he submitted a motion to reopen, and claimed ineffective assistance of counsel. He stated that he failed to appear due to the specific instruction from his counsel that he did not need to appear for that hearing because it would be continued. The respondent has explained his decision not to file a bar complaint. The issue on appeal is whether the respondent has substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent’s counsel had appeared at the hearing and verified that the respondent had notice of the hearing. She concluded that the respondent had not demonstrated substantial compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
According to the respondent’s affidavit, two different individuals from the law firm that continues to represent him told him he did not need to appear for the hearing on January 19, 2006. Both individuals named by the respondent, Mr. Duran and Mr. Ramos, have submitted entry of appearance forms indicating they were admitted to the bar in the state of California. Under these circumstances, and considering that Ms. Vargas confirmed on the day of the last hearing that the respondent had proper notice, we do not find this to be a situation where full compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, is unnecessary. It is not clear from the record who is to blame for the respondent’s failure to appear for a hearing for which he admittedly received proper notice from the Immigration Judge. If a properly licensed attorney told the respondent he need not appear under these circumstances, then compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, is necessary to assign responsibility for this ineffective assistance. The respondent’s suggestion that he received “erroneous instructions” which “do not merit a complaint to the State Bar” is unpersuasive because it is not merely the respondent’s prerogative as to whether or not to comply with Matter of Lozada, supra. Given the different attorneys who were apparently involved in the respondent’s representation, this is simply not a case where “the record shows a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance.” Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226-27 (9th Cir. 2002). See also Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000). The Immigration Judge appropriately denied reopening. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2006 WL 3088916 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: REYES-CHACON, MARCO ANTONIO
FILE: AXX-XX4-841 - SALT LAKE CITY
September 19, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Culas, Roberto G., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Matthew Hall,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 07, 2006. The record reflects that the respondent appeared before an Immigration Judge on November 8, 2005, and was allowed the opportunity to depart the United States voluntarily by March 8, 2006. On February 24, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge. Therein, the respondent indicates that his mother filed a visa petition on his behalf, and he is prima facie eligible to adjust his status to a lawful permanent resident of the United States. He further indicates in his affidavit that his former counsel may not have notified the Immigration Judge at the hearing of the respondent’s eligibility for relief from removal. He requested a new hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on March 7, 2006, and this appeal followed.
 
The respondent argues in his Notice of Appeal that his former counsel was negligent in these proceedings because counsel failed to advise the Immigration Judge at the hearing that he was eligible for adjustment of status. However, the respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely filed because it was submitted more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge’s November 8, 2005, order. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4). The respondent has also failed to indicate when he learned of his counsel’s alleged negligence. We therefore cannot find based on the evidence submitted that equitable tolling of the period for seeking reopening is appropriate.
 
In addition, the respondent fails to meet the requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Galvez Pineda v. Gonzales, 127 F.3d 833, 838-39 (10th Cir. 2005) (recognizing the requirements for filing ineffective assistance of counsel claims). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. The respondent has not complied with any of the procedural requirements for proving that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. It is therefore inappropriate to reopen these proceedings to allow the respondent a new hearing. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2006 WL 3088894 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MATEO SARABIA-BOJORQUEZ
FILE: A20 687 951 - EL PASO
A79 041 7811
September 18, 2006
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Amy W. Apostol, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Stephen M. Ruhle
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. In a decision dated April 26, 2006, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind the order of deportation that had been entered on May 14, 1984, in deportation proceedings which had been conducted in absentia under section 242(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252(b), due to the respondent’s failure to appear. The respondent filed a timely appeal from that decision. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that he had no knowledge that he had ever been placed under deportation proceedings. Moreover, he disclaims any knowledge of the attorney who represented him in those proceedings, and in any event, maintains that he did not receive notice of the deportation hearing scheduled for May 14, 1984, and that said attorney failed to notify him of that hearing and his actions constitute ineffective assistance of counsel, and establish reasonable cause for his failure to appear.
 
The respondent’s allegations are belied by the record. At the outset, we consider the respondent received personal service of the Order to Show Cause (form I-221S) on June 10, 1983, while he was detained at the El Paso Processing Center. The respondent’s fingerprint and signature were placed on the Order to Show Cause at the time it was served (Exh. 1). Furthermore, the record indicates the respondent retained the attorney Paul Douglass to represent him as evidenced by the Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney (Form G-28) filed on the respondent’s behalf and dated June 14, 1983 (Exh. 2). The respondent’s counsel, Paul Douglass, then filed a motion for bond redetermination dated June 14, 1983 (Exh. 3), and the respondent’s bond was reduced to $500 after a bond hearing held on June 14, 1983 (Exh. 4). On June 15, 1983, the respondent posted bond and was released from custody, and the respondent’s counsel, Paul Douglass, was the obligor for this bond (Exh. 5). Upon his release from custody, the respondent was served with a notice that his hearing had been rescheduled to an undetermined future date (Exh. 6). On June 30, 1983, the Immigration Court sent notice of the respondent’s rescheduled hearing for May 14, 1984, to his attorney Paul Douglass, by certified mail, return receipt requested (Exh. 7). This notice was accepted and signed for by the office of his attorney Paul Douglass on July 1, 1983 (Exh. 8). The record reflects the respondent failed to appear for the rescheduled hearing. See I.J. Dec. at 2. Since the respondent failed to appear for the hearing on May 14, 1984, he was ordered deported in absentia to Mexico (Exh. 9).
 
*2 We find that the Immigration Judge correctly concluded that the Immigration Court provided proper notice of the hearing to the respondent. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). Moreover, we find no support for the respondent’s contention that his attorney of record failed to inform him that he had a scheduled hearing which resulted in the order of deportation being entered in his absence.
 
We have held that to satisfy a claim based upon ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must support his claim by a detailed affidavit, inform his previous attorney of the allegations of misconduct and provide the attorney with an opportunity to respond, and state whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to the attorney’s alleged violation of ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Here, the respondent has failed to comply with any of the prongs described in Matter of Lozada, supra. Accordingly, we find that the respondent has not satisfied a Lozada showing of ineffective assistance of counsel. See also Matter of N-K- & V-S-, 21 I&N Dec. 879 (BIA 1997) (burden of proving ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
Consequently, we find that the respondent received proper notice of his hearing and that he has not provided any reasonable cause of his failure to appear. See Matter of Ruiz, 20 I&N Dec. 91 (BIA 1989); Matter of Haim, 19 I&N Dec. 641 (BIA 1988); Matter of Nafi, 19 I&N Dec. 430 (BIA 1987). The respondent has presented no arguments on appeal that would cause us to reverse the Immigration Judge’s decision. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

Both parties shall likewise modify their records accordingly and will ensure that the new alien registration number is used in any future correspondence involving this respondent.

2006 WL 3203596 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EVERETT EARL JACKSON
FILE: AXX XX7 540 - HOUSTON
September 15, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)º-Crime involving moral turpitude
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This matter was last before the Board on June 2, 2005, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s May 7, 2004, decision finding the respondent removable as charged, statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal, and denying his application for voluntary departure. The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica, on August 22, 2006, filed a motion to reopen. He acknowledges that the motion was not filed in a timely fashion but urges that the proceedings should be reopened by reason of his submission of evidence showing prima facie eligibility for relief as well as evidence which is material. He also requests reopening by reason of ineffective assistance of counsel, both during the proceedings before the Immigration Judge, and on appeal. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed not later than 90 days after the date that decision was rendered, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). In the instant case, the Board issued a decision on June 2, 2005. A motion to reopen would therefore have been due on or before Thursday, September 1, 2005. As the respondent acknowledges, the motion was untimely where it was filed on August 22, 2006, approximately 12 months after it was due. The respondent claims that the filing deadline should be ignored as he is eligible for relief and has submitted material evidence. However, those are the requirements which must be satisfied for the Board to grant a timely filed motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
In our precedent decision in Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), we emphasized the importance of the time limits on motions to reopen, noting the need to discourage dilatory motions and Congress’ mandate that we do so. We further recognized our limited discretionary power to reopen, as well as the need to exercise such power only in exceptional situations. Id., see also Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216 (BIA 2000). The burden is on the respondent to demonstrate that an exceptional situation exists. The respondent in this case has failed to meet that burden. The respondent identifies June 2, 2005, the date of the Board decision, as the last date of his claimed ineffective assistance of counsel. In that decision, we found that he had failed to meet the procedural requirements as set out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), and that he had failed to show prejudice. In his motion he fails to address why his untimely motion to reopen should be considered, given that nothing in his motion presents a circumstance which precluded him from timely filing his motion to reopen. The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed out of time, and we find that exercising our discretion to reopen this matter is not in order.
 
*2 To the extent that the respondent intended his “Motion to Reopen and Reconsider” to be considered a motion to reconsider, we point out that it is untimely. A motion to reconsider must be filed within 30 days after the mailing of the Board decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). The Board’s decision in this case was entered on June 2, 2005. Thus, a motion to reconsider would have been due on or before July 5, 2005. Accordingly, to the extent that we consider the respondent’s motion as a motion to reconsider, it must be considered to have been filed out of time.
 
Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2006 WL 3088913 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARLON BARRIOS-HERNANDEZ
FILE: AXX XX1 504 - EL CENTRO
September 12, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Roberto G. Culas, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jeff Lindblad
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler.
  
APPLICATION: Remand
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s June 1, 2006, decision ordering him removed from the United States as an alien who has knowingly assisted another alien to enter the United States in violation of law. See section 212(a)(6)(E)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i) (2000). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The sole issue raised on appeal is whether the attorney who represented the respondent during his proceedings before the Immigration Judge provided ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to file an asylum application on the respondent’s behalf and by failing to investigate whether the respondent was in fact removable as charged. We dismiss the respondent’s appeal for several reasons. As a threshold matter, the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), nor does the record show such a “clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance” as to warrant consideration of his claim despite his failure to comply with those requirements. Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 582, 587-88 (9th Cir. 2006) (quoting Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir. 2002); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir. 2000)). In this connection, we note that the respondent— while sworn—repeatedly admitted to the Immigration Judge that he had knowingly assisted another alien to enter the United States in violation of law, thereby supporting the charge of removability (Tr. at 15-16, 39-40). Moreover, although he initially stated that he had a fear of persecution or torture in El Salvador (Tr. at 17), he specifically disavowed that assertion—again, while sworn—in two subsequent hearings. (Tr. at 42, 45). Absent truly compelling circumstances not present here, an attorney does not provide ineffective assistance by declining to pursue lines of inquiry or forms of relief that have been expressly foreclosed by the sworn testimony of the client.
 
Finally, the respondent has not provided persuasive evidence of prejudice arising from counsel’s allegedly deficient performance, i.e, evidence reflecting a reasonable possibility that the outcome of his removal hearing would have been different had counsel provided more proficient representation. Ray v. Gonzales, supra, at 587; Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 824-26 (9th Cir. 2003). Specifically, the respondent has offered no evidence, either before the Immigration Judge or in connection with his appeal to this Board, to suggest that the charge of removability is unfounded or that he is in fact eligible for asylum despite his representations that he has no fear of persecution or torture in El Salvador. Although the respondent’s appellate brief contains a statement of counsel indicating that he conceded removability in order to protect his estranged spouse, who he claims was the real “culprit” in his alien smuggling activity, such statements by counsel are not evidence. Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980).
 
*2 Because the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is deficient on its face, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

End of Document

2006 WL 3088963 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CRISTIAN ANDERS NIETO-ZUBIETA
FILE: AXX XX5 760 - SALT LAKE CITY
September 8, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rev. Michael R. Sciumbato, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mindy E. Hoeppner
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 5, 2006, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. Subsequent to filing a notice of appeal, the respondent filed a motion to rescind and reopen with the Immigration Court on June 28, 2006. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States, following the respondent’s failure to appear for a hearing on January 31, 2006. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that he established exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear due to the disconnection of his cellular telephone, which resulted in the inability of the respondent’s former counsel to contact him regarding the new hearing date; and due to the ineffective assistance of former counsel. Specifically, the respondent contends that his former counsel failed to inform him in writing of his next scheduled hearing, despite the fact that his former counsel was aware of the respondent’s current address. The respondent has submitted additional evidence on appeal in support of his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has failed to establish exceptional circumstances based on his claim that his cellular telephone was disconnected, and therefore, his former attorney was unable to contact him on his cellular telephone to inform him of the new hearing date. The respondent failed to provide any evidence regarding the dates of the alleged disconnection or the reason for the disconnection. Moreover, the respondent’s claim of exceptional circumstances due to disconnection of a cellular telephone does not qualify as an “exceptional circumstance” as defined section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act.
 
On appeal the respondent is represented by new counsel and now alleges ineffective assistance of counsel as the basis for his failure to appear. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) are satisfied. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent has submitted additional evidence on appeal, including his affidavit, a letter to his former attorney informing him of the allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel, and a complaint letter to the Utah State Bar Disciplinary Committee. The Board ordinarily will not consider any previously available evidence first proffered on appeal, as its review is limited to the record of proceedings before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995). However, we find such evidence probative of the respondent’s claim of exceptional circumstances due to ineffective assistance of counsel and will remand the record to the Immigration Judge for consideration of this issue only. Accordingly, the record will be remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.
 
*2 ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.

2006 WL 3088950 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NICOLAS VELAZQUEZ-GONZALEZ
FILE: AXX XX4 575 - PORTLAND
September 8, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Aaron Clingerman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Thomas L. Day
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i)º - Conditional resident status terminated
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on June 10, 2005, when we affirmed, without opinion, the Immigration Judge’s March 11, 2004, decision denying the respondent’s application for adjustment of status. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(4).
 
On March 29, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to reopen these proceedings to afford him the opportunity to apply for adjustment of status. He also asks for a stay of voluntary departure.
 
The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the respondent’s motion. It asserts that the respondent’s motion is not supported by material, previously unavailable evidence that supports reopening. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (a motion to reopen proceedings shall not be granted unless the evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing). The DHS also asserts that the respondent’s underlying approved immediate relative visa petition was automatically revoked when the petitioner, the respondent’s United States citizen spouse, died. See 8 C.F.R. § 205.1 (a)(3)(i)(C). Consequently, it asserts that the respondent is no longer statutorily eligible for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i).
 
Initially, we observe that the respondent’s March 29, 2006, motion to reopen is time barred under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (a party may file only one motion to reopen proceedings, whether before the Board or the Immigration Judge, and that motion must be filed no later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceeding sought to be reopened). A final administrative order was entered in this case on June 10, 2005.
 
The respondent argues that the motions deadline should be equitably tolled based on his claim of ineffective assistance by his former counsel, Mr. James White. The respondent asserts that Mr. White failed to inform him of the status of his appeal and of his right to file a motion to reopen. The respondent has provided a declaration in which he claims that he first learned that this Board dismissed his appeal on February 10, 2006, during an interview with DHS officers. See Respondent’s Motion at 2. The respondent also correctly asserts that Mr. White was suspended from the practice of law in Oregon effective December 10, 2005, and was suspended from practice before this Board and the Immigration Courts effective January 11, 2006. See Decision of the Board of Immigration Appeals dated January 11, 2006. The respondent states that complete compliance with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), is unnecessary where, as here, the ineffectiveness is clear from the record.
 
*2 Equitable tolling of the time limits for motions to reopen is available to aliens who have filed with the Board otherwise untimely motions in which they raise allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (holding that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error). We find that equitable tolling is appropriate in this case where the respondent substantially complied with the Lozada requirements, established that his case was prejudiced as a result of Mr. White’s misconduct, and, diligently filed this motion to reopen within a month of learning that his appeal was dismissed.
 
We now turn to the merits of the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent seeks reopening to apply for adjustment of status. The DHS asserts that the respondent is no longer eligible for adjustment because his underlying visa petition was automatically revoked on the death of his United States citizen spouse.
 
However, pursuant to section 201(b)(2)(A)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1151(b)(2)(A)(i), if the alien was the spouse of a United States citizen for two years before the citizen’s death and was not legally separated from the citizen at the time of the citizen’s death, the alien shall be considered to remain an immediate relative after the date of the citizen’s death but only if the spouse files a petition under 204(a)(1)(A)(ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1154(a)(1)(A)(ii), with in two years after such date and only until the date the spouse remarries. Although it is unclear when the respondent’s wife died, it appears that the respondent may be eligible for file a visa petition under this provision.
 
The respondent was granted a period of voluntary departure by this Board pursuant to section 240B(b) of the Act. Such period of voluntary departure ended on August 9, 2005. The respondent failed to depart the United States during such time period. In most cases, an alien who fails to depart the United States within the time period specified in a voluntary departure order is, by virtue of that failure, rendered ineligible for adjustment of status for a period of 10 years. See section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d). In this case, however, respondent’s failure to voluntarily depart or file a timely motion within the voluntary departure period was due to the clear ineffectiveness assistance of counsel. Accordingly, we find that the respondent is not barred from relief by operation of section 240B(d) of the Act. See generally Azarte v. Ashcroft, 394 F.3d 1278 (9th Cir. 2005) (holding “that in cases in which a motion to reopen is filed within the voluntary departure period and a stay of removal or voluntary departure is requested, the voluntary departure period is tolled during the period the ¿Boardº is considering the motion”).
 
*3 Based on the foregoing we find it appropriate to grant the respondent’s motion to reopen and remand these proceedings to the Immigration Judge so that the respondent can apply for adjustment of status and any other form of relief from removal for which he can establish eligibility. The appropriate orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2006 WL 3088950 (BIA)
End of Document

2006 WL 3088952 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VASQUEZ-CHAVIRA, JAVIER
FILE: A78-895-543 - DENVER
September 8, 2006
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Duane Montano, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Bonnie K. Brady,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen the proceeding to apply for adjustment of status based on his status as the spouse of a deceased United States citizen as provided in section 201(b)(2)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1151(b)(2).1 The appeal will be dismissed.
 
An Immigration Judge has limited authority to hear an application for adjustment of status in removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(1). Relevant to the instant matter, the regulations provide that although there is no direct appeal from the decision of the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) to deny an adjustment application, the “applicant, if not an arriving alien, retains the right to renew his or her application” in removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(5).
 
The record reflects that by notice (Form I- 191) dated December 5, 2002, the district direct of the Immigration and Naturalization Service (now the DHS) denied the respondent’s application for adjustment of status.2 He is not now, however, seeking to renew that application; rather, the respondent has ignored the renewal procedure authorized in the regulations and is seeking reopening to apply for adjustment of status based on an entirely different visa petition. See Matter of Lasike, 17 I&N Dec. 445 (BIA 1980) (holding that an application may be considered “renewed” only when based on the same facts and reviewed according to circumstances as they existed when the original application was filed). We find no legal authority in the Act or in the regulations, and the respondent has not provided any legal authority, that permits him to seek adjustment of status based on a self-petition where, as here, the alien is in removal proceedings and a previously filed adjustment application has been denied by the DHS.
 
In any event, the respondent failed to support his motion to reopen with a completed application for adjustment of status (Form I-485) with supporting evidentiary material. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The respondent has not shown that his self-petition has been approved or that he is the beneficiary of an approved visa petition filed by his now deceased United States citizen wife. See section 245 of the Act; Matter of Perez Vargas, 23 I&N Dec. 829 (BIA 2005) (The DHS has sole authority to adjudicate a visa petition.). Further, he has not provided any evidentiary material to show the likelihood that he will succeed in demonstrating the statutory requirements for a self-petition under section 201(b)(2)(A)(i) of the Act. Consequently, the respondent has failed to provide prima facie proof of eligibility for adjustment of status under the Act. See Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413 (BIA 1996); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992); Matter of Sipus, 14 I&N Dec. 229 (BIA 1972).
 
*2 The respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge violated his right to due process by failing to inform him of the relief available to him during the removal hearing is foreclosed by United States v. Aguirre-Tello, 353 F.3d 1199 (10th Cir. 2004). In that decision, the United States Court of Appeals for the Tenth Circuit, the circuit in which this cases arises, decided that a deportable alien does not have a constitutional right to be informed of discretionary relief that might be available to him or her. See id., at 1204. To the extent that the respondent is asserting an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his former counsel for “inexcusable neglect,” the claim fails because the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

2

According to the Form I-191, the respondent failed to provide information concerning his convictions on February 28, 2001, and on March 18, 2002, for domestic abuse of his now deceased United States citizen wife. The record contains a copy of the February 12, 2004, judgment of the Municipal Court, City of Englewood, Arapaphoe Country, Colorado, in which the court vacated the February 28, 2001, conviction.
